<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sources">Sources</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:AGMA_H%C3%A9rodote.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/AGMA_H%C3%A9rodote.jpg/180px-AGMA_H%C3%A9rodote.jpg" decoding="async" width="180" height="257" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/AGMA_H%C3%A9rodote.jpg/270px-AGMA_H%C3%A9rodote.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/AGMA_H%C3%A9rodote.jpg/360px-AGMA_H%C3%A9rodote.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="1714" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:AGMA_H%C3%A9rodote.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a>, the main historical source for this conflict</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thucydides-bust-cutout_ROM.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Thucydides-bust-cutout_ROM.jpg/180px-Thucydides-bust-cutout_ROM.jpg" decoding="async" width="180" height="313" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Thucydides-bust-cutout_ROM.jpg/270px-Thucydides-bust-cutout_ROM.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Thucydides-bust-cutout_ROM.jpg/360px-Thucydides-bust-cutout_ROM.jpg 2x" data-file-width="998" data-file-height="1735" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thucydides-bust-cutout_ROM.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Thucydides" title="Thucydides">Thucydides</a> continued Herodotus's narrative</div></div></div>
<p>Almost all the primary sources for the Greco-Persian Wars are Greek; there are no surviving historical accounts from the Persian side. This is due to the fact that Alexander the Great, who was the second in history to be given that title after Cyrus the Great, burned the library of Persepolis. By some distance, the main source for the Greco-Persian Wars is the Greek historian <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a>. Herodotus, who has been called the "Father of History",<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> was born in 484 BC in <a href="/wiki/Halicarnassus" title="Halicarnassus">Halicarnassus</a>, Asia Minor (then part of the Persian empire). He wrote his 'Enquiries' (Greek <i>Historia</i>, English <i><a href="/wiki/Histories_(Herodotus)" title="Histories (Herodotus)">(The) Histories</a></i>) around 440–430 BC, trying to trace the origins of the Greco-Persian Wars, which would still have been recent history.<sup id="cite_ref-hxvi_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hxvi-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Herodotus's approach was novel and, at least in Western society, he invented 'history' as a discipline.<sup id="cite_ref-hxvi_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hxvi-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> As historian <a href="/wiki/Tom_Holland_(author)" title="Tom Holland (author)">Tom Holland</a> has it, "For the first time, a chronicler set himself to trace the origins of a conflict not to a past so remote so as to be utterly fabulous, nor to the whims and wishes of some god, nor to a people's claim to manifest destiny, but rather explanations he could verify personally."<sup id="cite_ref-hxvi_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hxvi-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some later ancient historians, starting with <a href="/wiki/Thucydides" title="Thucydides">Thucydides</a>, criticized Herodotus and his methods.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fin15_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fin15-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, Thucydides chose to begin his history where Herodotus left off (at the <a href="#Sestos">Siege of Sestos</a>) and felt Herodotus's history was accurate enough not to need re-writing or correcting.<sup id="cite_ref-Fin15_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fin15-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a> criticised Herodotus in his essay "On The Malignity of Herodotus", describing Herodotus as "<i>Philobarbaros</i>" (barbarian-lover) for not being pro-Greek enough, which suggests that Herodotus might actually have done a reasonable job of being even-handed.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> A negative view of Herodotus was passed on to Renaissance Europe, though he remained well read. However, since the 19th century, his reputation has been dramatically rehabilitated by archaeological finds that have repeatedly confirmed his version of events.<sup id="cite_ref-h377_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h377-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The prevailing modern view is that Herodotus did a remarkable job in his <i>Historia</i>, but that some of his specific details (particularly troop numbers and dates) should be viewed with skepticism.<sup id="cite_ref-h377_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h377-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, there are still some historians who believe Herodotus made up much of his story.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The military history of Greece between the end of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece" title="Second Persian invasion of Greece">second Persian invasion of Greece</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Peloponnesian_War" title="Peloponnesian War">Peloponnesian War</a> (479–431 BC) is not well supported by surviving ancient sources. This period, sometimes referred to as the <i><a href="/wiki/Pentekontaetia" class="mw-redirect" title="Pentekontaetia">pentekontaetia</a></i> (<i>πεντηκονταετία</i>, <i>the Fifty Years</i>) by ancient writers, was a period of relative peace and prosperity within Greece.<sup id="cite_ref-Fin16_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fin16-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kag77_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kag77-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> The richest source for the period, and also the most contemporaneous, is Thucydides' <i><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Peloponnesian_War" title="History of the Peloponnesian War">History of the Peloponnesian War</a></i>, which is generally considered by modern historians to be a reliable primary account.<sup id="cite_ref-Sea264_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sea264-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fine336_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fine336-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fin29_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fin29-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Thucydides only mentions this period in a digression on the growth of Athenian power in the run up to the Peloponnesian War, and the account is brief, probably selective and lacks any dates.<sup id="cite_ref-Sea248*_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sea248*-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fine343_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fine343-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, Thucydides's account can be, and is, used by historians to draw up a skeleton chronology for the period, on to which details from archaeological records and other writers can be superimposed.<sup id="cite_ref-Sea248*_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sea248*-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>More detail for the whole period is provided by Plutarch, in his <a href="/wiki/Parallel_Lives" title="Parallel Lives">biographies</a> of <a href="/wiki/Themistocles" title="Themistocles">Themistocles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aristides" title="Aristides">Aristides</a> and especially <a href="/wiki/Cimon" title="Cimon">Cimon</a>. Plutarch was writing some 600 years after the events in question, and is therefore a secondary source, but he often names his sources, which allows some degree of verification of his statements.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> In his biographies, he draws directly from many ancient histories that have not survived, and thus often preserves details of the period that are omitted in Herodotus and Thucydides's accounts. The final major existing source for the period is the universal history (<i><a href="/wiki/Bibliotheca_historica" title="Bibliotheca historica">Bibliotheca historica</a></i>) of the 1st century BC Sicilian, <a href="/wiki/Diodorus_Siculus" title="Diodorus Siculus">Diodorus Siculus</a>. Much of Diodorus's writing about this period is drawn from the much earlier Greek historian <a href="/wiki/Ephorus" title="Ephorus">Ephorus</a>, who also wrote a universal history.<sup id="cite_ref-Fine360_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fine360-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Diodorus is also a secondary source and often derided by modern historians for his style and inaccuracies, but he preserves many details of the ancient period found nowhere else.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Further scattered details can be found in <a href="/wiki/Pausanias_(geographer)" title="Pausanias (geographer)">Pausanias</a>'s <i>Description of Greece</i>, while the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine</a> <a href="/wiki/Suda" title="Suda">Suda</a> dictionary of the 10th century AD preserves some anecdotes found nowhere else. Minor sources for the period include the works of <a href="/wiki/Gnaeus_Pompeius_Trogus" title="Gnaeus Pompeius Trogus">Pompeius Trogus</a> (epitomized by <a href="/wiki/Justin_(historian)" title="Justin (historian)">Justinus</a>), <a href="/wiki/Cornelius_Nepos" title="Cornelius Nepos">Cornelius Nepos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ctesias" title="Ctesias">Ctesias of Cnidus</a> (epitomized by <a href="/wiki/Photios_I_of_Constantinople" title="Photios I of Constantinople">Photius</a>), which are not in their original textual form. These works are not considered reliable (especially Ctesias), and are not particularly useful for reconstructing the history of this period.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins_of_the_conflict">Origins of the conflict</span></h2>
<p>The Greeks of the classical period believed that, in the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Dark_Ages" title="Greek Dark Ages">dark age</a> that followed the collapse of the <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greece" title="Mycenaean Greece">Mycenaean civilization</a>, significant numbers of Greeks fled and  had emigrated to Asia Minor and settled there.<sup id="cite_ref-I14x_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-I14x-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Modern historians generally accept this migration as historic (but separate from the later colonization of the Mediterranean by the Greeks).<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> There are, however, those who believe the Ionian migration cannot be explained as simply as the classical Greeks claimed.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>  These settlers were from three tribal groups: the <a href="/wiki/Aeolians" title="Aeolians">Aeolians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dorians" title="Dorians">Dorians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ionians" title="Ionians">Ionians</a>. The Ionians had settled about the coasts of <a href="/wiki/Lydia" title="Lydia">Lydia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Caria" title="Caria">Caria</a>, founding the twelve cities that made up <a href="/wiki/Ionia" title="Ionia">Ionia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-I14x_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-I14x-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> These cities were <a href="/wiki/Miletus" title="Miletus">Miletus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Myus" title="Myus">Myus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Priene" title="Priene">Priene</a> in Caria; <a href="/wiki/Ephesus" title="Ephesus">Ephesus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colophon_(city)" title="Colophon (city)">Colophon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lebedos" class="mw-redirect" title="Lebedos">Lebedos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Teos" title="Teos">Teos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clazomenae" class="mw-redirect" title="Clazomenae">Clazomenae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phocaea" title="Phocaea">Phocaea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Erythrae" title="Erythrae">Erythrae</a> in Lydia; and the islands of <a href="/wiki/Samos" title="Samos">Samos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chios" title="Chios">Chios</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-I142_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-I142-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Although the Ionian cities were independent of one another, they recognized their shared heritage and supposedly had a common temple and meeting place, the <i>Panionion</i>.<sup id="ref_ii" class="reference"><a href="#cnote_ii">&#91;ii&#93;</a></sup> They thus formed a 'cultural league', to which they would admit no other cities, or even other tribal Ionians.<sup id="cite_ref-I143_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-I143-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-I148_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-I148-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The cities of Ionia remained independent until they were conquered by the <a href="/wiki/Lydians" title="Lydians">Lydians</a> of western Asia Minor. The Lydian king <a href="/wiki/Alyattes_of_Lydia" title="Alyattes of Lydia">Alyattes</a> attacked Miletus, a conflict that ended with a treaty of alliance between Miletus and Lydia, that meant that Miletus would have internal autonomy but follow Lydia in foreign affairs.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> At this time, the Lydians were also in conflict with the <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">Median</a> Empire, and the Milesians sent an army to aid the Lydians in this conflict. Eventually a peaceable settlement was established between the Medes and the Lydians, with the <a href="/wiki/Halys_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Halys River">Halys River</a> set up as the border between the kingdoms.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> The famous Lydian king <a href="/wiki/Croesus" title="Croesus">Croesus</a> succeeded his father Alyattes in around 560 BC and set about conquering the other Greek city states of Asia Minor.<sup id="cite_ref-I126_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-I126-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Persia" class="mw-redirect" title="Persia">Persian</a> prince <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">Cyrus</a> led a rebellion against the last Median king <a href="/wiki/Astyages" title="Astyages">Astyages</a> in 553 BC. Cyrus was a grandson of Astyages and was supported by part of the Median aristocracy.<sup id="cite_ref-h9_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h9-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> By 550 BC, the rebellion was over, and Cyrus had emerged victorious, founding the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid Empire</a> in place of the Median kingdom in the process.<sup id="cite_ref-h9_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h9-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Croesus saw the disruption in the Median Empire and Persia as an opportunity to extend his realm and asked the <a href="/wiki/Delphic_Oracle" class="mw-redirect" title="Delphic Oracle">oracle</a> of <a href="/wiki/Delphi" title="Delphi">Delphi</a> whether he should attack them. The Oracle supposedly replied the famously ambiguous answer that "if Croesus was to cross the Halys he would destroy a great empire".<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Blind to the ambiguity of this prophecy, Croesus attacked the Persians, but was eventually defeated and Lydia fell to Cyrus.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> By crossing the Halys, Croesus had indeed destroyed a great empire – his own.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Achaemenid_Empire_under_different_kings_(flat_map).svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Achaemenid_Empire_under_different_kings_%28flat_map%29.svg/300px-Achaemenid_Empire_under_different_kings_%28flat_map%29.svg.png" decoding="async" width="300" height="197" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Achaemenid_Empire_under_different_kings_%28flat_map%29.svg/450px-Achaemenid_Empire_under_different_kings_%28flat_map%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Achaemenid_Empire_under_different_kings_%28flat_map%29.svg/600px-Achaemenid_Empire_under_different_kings_%28flat_map%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="672" data-file-height="442" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Achaemenid_Empire_under_different_kings_(flat_map).svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Achaemenid Empire at its greatest extent under <a href="/wiki/Darius_the_Great" title="Darius the Great">Darius the Great</a></div></div></div>
<p>While fighting the Lydians, Cyrus had sent messages to the Ionians asking them to revolt against Lydian rule, which the Ionians had refused to do. After Cyrus finished the conquest of Lydia, the Ionian cities now offered to be his subjects under the same terms as they had been subjects of Croesus.<sup id="cite_ref-I141_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-I141-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Cyrus refused, citing the Ionians' unwillingness to help him previously. The Ionians thus prepared to defend themselves, and Cyrus sent the Median general <a href="/wiki/Harpagus" title="Harpagus">Harpagus</a> to conquer them.<sup id="cite_ref-I163_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-I163-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> He first attacked Phocaea; the Phocaeans decided to abandon their city entirely  and sail into exile in Sicily, rather than become Persian subjects (although many later returned).<sup id="cite_ref-I164_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-I164-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Some Teians also chose to emigrate when Harpagus attacked Teos, but the rest of the Ionians remained, and were each in turn conquered.<sup id="cite_ref-I169_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-I169-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the years following their conquest, the Persians found the Ionians difficult to rule. Elsewhere in the empire, Cyrus identified elite native groups such as the priesthood of Judea – to help him rule his new subjects. No such group existed in Greek cities at this time; while there was usually an aristocracy, this was inevitably divided into feuding factions. The Persians thus settled for sponsoring a tyrant in each Ionian city, even though this drew them into the Ionians' internal conflicts. Furthermore, certain tyrants might develop an independent streak and have to be replaced. The tyrants themselves faced a difficult task; they had to deflect the worst of their fellow citizens' hatred, while staying in the favour of the Persians.<sup id="cite_ref-h147_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h147-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> In the past, Greek states had often been ruled by tyrants, but that form of government was on the decline. Past tyrants had also tended and needed to be strong and able leaders, whereas the rulers appointed by the Persians were simply place-men. Backed by Persian military might, these tyrants did not need the support of the population, and could thus rule absolutely.<sup id="cite_ref-fine_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fine-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> On the eve of the Greco-Persian wars, it is probable that the Ionian population had become discontented and was ready for rebellion.<sup id="cite_ref-h155_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h155-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Warfare_in_the_ancient_Mediterranean">Warfare in the ancient Mediterranean</span></h3>
<p>In the Greco-Persian wars both sides made use of spear-armed infantry and light missile troops. Greek armies placed the emphasis on heavier infantry, while Persian armies favoured lighter troop types.<sup id="cite_ref-L23_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-L23-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-L256_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-L256-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Persia">Persia</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pers%C3%A9polis._La_Garde.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Pers%C3%A9polis._La_Garde.jpg/300px-Pers%C3%A9polis._La_Garde.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="198" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Pers%C3%A9polis._La_Garde.jpg/450px-Pers%C3%A9polis._La_Garde.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Pers%C3%A9polis._La_Garde.jpg/600px-Pers%C3%A9polis._La_Garde.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1675" data-file-height="1107" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pers%C3%A9polis._La_Garde.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Persian and Median <a href="/wiki/Immortals_(Achaemenid_Empire)" title="Immortals (Achaemenid Empire)">Immortals</a> in ceremonial dress, bas-relief in <a href="/wiki/Persepolis" title="Persepolis">Persepolis</a></div></div></div>
<p>The Persian military consisted of a diverse group of men drawn across the various nations of the empire. However, according to Herodotus, there was at least a general conformity in armor and style of fighting.<sup id="cite_ref-L23_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-L23-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> The troops were usually armed with a bow, a 'short spear' and a sword or axe, and carried a wicker shield. They wore a leather jerkin,<sup id="cite_ref-L23_46-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-L23-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> although individuals of high status wore high-quality metal armor. The Persians most likely used their bows to wear down the enemy, then closed in to deliver the final blow with spears and swords.<sup id="cite_ref-L23_46-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-L23-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> The first rank of Persian infantry formations, the so-called '<a href="/wiki/Sparabara" title="Sparabara">sparabara</a>', had no bows, carried larger wicker shields and were sometimes armed with longer spears. Their role was to protect the back ranks of the formation.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> The cavalry probably fought as lightly armed missile cavalry.<sup id="cite_ref-L23_46-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-L23-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Greece">Greece</span></h4>
<p>The style of warfare between the Greek city-states, which dates back until at least 650 BC (as dated by the '<a href="/wiki/Chigi_vase" title="Chigi vase">Chigi vase</a>'), was based around the <a href="/wiki/Hoplite" title="Hoplite">hoplite</a> <a href="/wiki/Phalanx_formation" class="mw-redirect" title="Phalanx formation">phalanx</a> supported by missile troops.<sup id="cite_ref-L256_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-L256-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-h69_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h69-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> The '<a href="/wiki/Hoplite" title="Hoplite">hoplites</a>' were foot soldiers usually drawn from the members of the middle-classes (in Athens called the <i>zeugites</i>), who could afford the equipment necessary to fight in this manner.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> The heavy armour usually included a breastplate or a <a href="/wiki/Linothorax" title="Linothorax">linothorax</a>, greaves, a helmet, and a large round, concave shield (the <i><a href="/wiki/Aspis" title="Aspis">aspis</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hoplon" class="mw-redirect" title="Hoplon">hoplon</a></i>).<sup id="cite_ref-L256_47-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-L256-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Hoplites were armed with long spears (the <i><a href="/wiki/Dory_(spear)" title="Dory (spear)">dory</a></i>), which were significantly longer than Persian spears, and a sword (the <i><a href="/wiki/Xiphos" title="Xiphos">xiphos</a></i>). The heavy armour and longer spears made them superior in hand-to-hand combat and gave them significant protection against ranged attacks.<sup id="cite_ref-L256_47-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-L256-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Lightly armed skirmishers, the <a href="/wiki/Psiloi" title="Psiloi">psiloi</a> also comprised a part of Greek armies growing in importance during the conflict; at the Battle of Plataea, for instance, they may have formed over half the Greek army.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Use of cavalry in Greek armies is not reported in the battles of the Greco-Persian Wars.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Naval_warfare">Naval warfare</span></h4>
<p>At the beginning of the conflict, all naval forces in the eastern Mediterranean had switched to the <a href="/wiki/Trireme" title="Trireme">trireme</a>, a warship powered by three banks of oars. The most common naval tactics during the period were ramming (Greek triremes were equipped with a cast-bronze ram at the bows), or boarding by ship-borne marines. More experienced naval powers had by this time also begun to use a manoeuver known as <i>diekplous</i>. It is not clear what this was, but it probably involved sailing into gaps between enemy ships and then ramming them in the side.<sup id="cite_ref-L34_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-L34-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Persian naval forces were primarily provided by the seafaring people of the empire: <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egyptians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cilicia" title="Cilicia">Cilicians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_history_of_Cyprus" title="Ancient history of Cyprus">Cypriots</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-VII89_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VII89-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-VI9_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VI9-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Other coastal regions of the Persian Empire would contribute ships throughout the course of the wars.<sup id="cite_ref-VII89_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VII89-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Preliminary_contacts_between_Persia_and_mainland_Greece_.28507_BC.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Preliminary_contacts_between_Persia_and_mainland_Greece_(507_BC)">Preliminary contacts between Persia and mainland Greece (507 BC)</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ceremony_of_Presenting_Earth_and_Water.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Ceremony_of_Presenting_Earth_and_Water.jpg/220px-Ceremony_of_Presenting_Earth_and_Water.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="136" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Ceremony_of_Presenting_Earth_and_Water.jpg/330px-Ceremony_of_Presenting_Earth_and_Water.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Ceremony_of_Presenting_Earth_and_Water.jpg/440px-Ceremony_of_Presenting_Earth_and_Water.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1277" data-file-height="791" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ceremony_of_Presenting_Earth_and_Water.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>According to <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a>, the Athenians, hoping for protection against <a href="/wiki/Sparta" title="Sparta">Sparta</a>,  made the gift of "<a href="/wiki/Earth_and_Water" class="mw-redirect" title="Earth and Water">Earth and Water</a>" to the Persians in 507 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-penelope.uchicago.edu_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-penelope.uchicago.edu-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ATTICA,_Athens._Circa_510_to_500-490_BC.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/ATTICA%2C_Athens._Circa_510_to_500-490_BC.jpg/220px-ATTICA%2C_Athens._Circa_510_to_500-490_BC.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/ATTICA%2C_Athens._Circa_510_to_500-490_BC.jpg/330px-ATTICA%2C_Athens._Circa_510_to_500-490_BC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/ATTICA%2C_Athens._Circa_510_to_500-490_BC.jpg/440px-ATTICA%2C_Athens._Circa_510_to_500-490_BC.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="452" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ATTICA,_Athens._Circa_510_to_500-490_BC.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_coinage" title="Ancient Greek coinage">Coinage of Athens</a> at the time of <a href="/wiki/Cleisthenes" title="Cleisthenes">Cleisthenes</a>. Effigy of <a href="/wiki/Athena" title="Athena">Athena</a>, with owl and ΑΘΕ, initials of "<a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a>". Circa 510-500/490 BC.</div></div></div>
<p>In 507 BC, <a href="/wiki/Artaphernes" title="Artaphernes">Artaphernes</a>, as brother of <a href="/wiki/Darius_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Darius I">Darius I</a> and Satrap of <a href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a> in his capital <a href="/wiki/Sardis" title="Sardis">Sardis</a>, received an embassy from newly democratic <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a>, probably sent by <a href="/wiki/Cleisthenes" title="Cleisthenes">Cleisthenes</a>, which was looking for Persian assistance in order to resist the threats from <a href="/wiki/Sparta" title="Sparta">Sparta</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-MW84_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MW84-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MW123_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MW123-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> reports that Artaphernes had no previous knowledge of the Athenians, and his initial reaction was "Who are these people?".<sup id="cite_ref-MW84_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MW84-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Artaphernes asked the Athenians for "Water and Earth", a symbol of submission, if they wanted help from the Achaemenid king.<sup id="cite_ref-MW123_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MW123-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> The Athenians ambassadors apparently accepted to comply, and to give "Earth and Water".<sup id="cite_ref-MW84_58-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MW84-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Artaphernes also advised the Athenians that they should receive back the Athenian <a href="/wiki/Tyrant" title="Tyrant">tyrant</a> <a href="/wiki/Hippias_(son_of_Pisistratus)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hippias (son of Pisistratus)">Hippias</a>.  The Persians threatened to attack Athens if they did not accept Hippias. Nevertheless, the Athenians preferred to remain democratic despite the danger from Persia, and the ambassadors were disavowed and censured upon their return to Athens.<sup id="cite_ref-MW84_58-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MW84-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The Athenians despatched envoys to Sardis, desiring to make an alliance with the Persians; for they knew that they had provoked the <a href="/wiki/Lacedaemonians" class="mw-redirect" title="Lacedaemonians">Lacedaemonians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cleomenes_I" title="Cleomenes I">Cleomenes</a> to war. When the envoys came to <a href="/wiki/Sardis" title="Sardis">Sardis</a> and spoke as they had been bidden, <a href="/wiki/Artaphrenes" class="mw-redirect" title="Artaphrenes">Artaphrenes</a> son of <a href="/wiki/Hystaspes_(father_of_Darius_I)" title="Hystaspes (father of Darius I)">Hystaspes</a>, viceroy of Sardis, asked them, "What men are you, and where dwell you, who desire alliance with the Persians?" Being informed by the envoys, he gave them an answer whereof the substance was, that if the Athenians gave king <a href="/wiki/Darius_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Darius I">Darius</a> <a href="/wiki/Earth_and_water" title="Earth and water">earth and water</a>, then he would make alliance with them; but if not, his command was that they should begone. The envoys consulted together and consented to give what was asked, in their desire to make the alliance. So they returned to their own country, and were then greatly blamed for what they had done.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite>Herodotus 5.73.<sup id="cite_ref-penelope.uchicago.edu_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-penelope.uchicago.edu-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>There is a possibility that the Achaemenid ruler now saw the Athenians as subjects who had solemnly promissed submission through the gift of "Earth and Water", and that subsequent actions by the Athenians, such as their intervention in the Ionian revolt, were perceived as a break of oath, and a rebellion to the central authority of the Achaemenid ruler.<sup id="cite_ref-MW84_58-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MW84-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Ionian_Revolt_.28499.E2.80.93493_BC.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Ionian_Revolt_(499–493_BC)">Ionian Revolt (499–493 BC)</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ionian_Revolt" title="Ionian Revolt">Ionian Revolt</a></div>
<div role="navigation" class="navbox" aria-labelledby="Ionian_Revolt" style="margin:0;float:right;clear:right;width:25.5em;margin-bottom:0.5em;margin-left:1em;;padding:3px"><table class="nowraplinks navbox-vertical mw-collapsible autocollapse navbox-inner" style="border-spacing:0;background:transparent;color:inherit"><tbody><tr><th scope="col" class="navbox-title" colspan="2" style="background-color:#C3D6EF;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Campaignbox_Ionian_Revolt" title="Template:Campaignbox Ionian Revolt"><abbr title="View this template" style=";background-color:#C3D6EF;;background:none transparent;border:none;-moz-box-shadow:none;-webkit-box-shadow:none;box-shadow:none; padding:0;">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Campaignbox_Ionian_Revolt" title="Template talk:Campaignbox Ionian Revolt"><abbr title="Discuss this template" style=";background-color:#C3D6EF;;background:none transparent;border:none;-moz-box-shadow:none;-webkit-box-shadow:none;box-shadow:none; padding:0;">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Campaignbox_Ionian_Revolt&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template" style=";background-color:#C3D6EF;;background:none transparent;border:none;-moz-box-shadow:none;-webkit-box-shadow:none;box-shadow:none; padding:0;">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div><div id="Ionian_Revolt" style="font-size:114%;margin:0 4em"><span style="line-height:1.6em"><a href="/wiki/Ionian_Revolt" title="Ionian Revolt">Ionian Revolt</a></span></div></th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="navbox-list navbox-odd hlist" style="width:100%;padding:0px"><div style="padding:0em 0.25em">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Naxos_(499_BC)" title="Siege of Naxos (499 BC)">Naxos</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ionian_Revolt#Sardis" title="Ionian Revolt">Sardis </a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ionian_Revolt#Battle_of_Ephesus" title="Ionian Revolt">Ephesus</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ionian_Revolt#Cyprus" title="Ionian Revolt">Cyprus</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ionian_Revolt#Battle_of_the_Marsyas" title="Ionian Revolt">Marsyas</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ionian_Revolt#Battle_of_Labraunda" title="Ionian Revolt">Labraunda</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ionian_Revolt#Battle_of_Pedasus" title="Ionian Revolt">Pedasus</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Lade" title="Battle of Lade">Lade</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ionian_Revolt#Fall_of_Miletus" title="Ionian Revolt">Miletus</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ionian_Revolt#Chios" title="Ionian Revolt">Chios</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ionian_Revolt#Battle_of_Malene" title="Ionian Revolt">Malene</a></li></ul>
</div></td></tr></tbody></table></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_burning_of_Sardis_during_the_Ionian_Revolt.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/The_burning_of_Sardis_during_the_Ionian_Revolt.jpg/220px-The_burning_of_Sardis_during_the_Ionian_Revolt.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="333" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/The_burning_of_Sardis_during_the_Ionian_Revolt.jpg/330px-The_burning_of_Sardis_during_the_Ionian_Revolt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/The_burning_of_Sardis_during_the_Ionian_Revolt.jpg/440px-The_burning_of_Sardis_during_the_Ionian_Revolt.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1054" data-file-height="1594" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_burning_of_Sardis_during_the_Ionian_Revolt.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The burning of Sardis by the Greeks and the Ionians during the Ionian Revolt in 498 BC.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ionian_Revolt" title="Ionian Revolt">Ionian Revolt</a> and associated revolts in <a href="/wiki/Aeolis" title="Aeolis">Aeolis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Doris_(Asia_Minor)" class="mw-redirect" title="Doris (Asia Minor)">Doris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Caria" title="Caria">Caria</a> were military rebellions by several regions of <a href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a> against Persian rule, lasting from 499 to 493 BC. At the heart of the rebellion was the dissatisfaction of the Greek cities of Asia Minor with the tyrants appointed by Persia to rule them, along with opposition to the individual actions of two Milesian tyrants, <a href="/wiki/Histiaeus" title="Histiaeus">Histiaeus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aristagoras" title="Aristagoras">Aristagoras</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-h147_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h147-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-h153_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h153-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> In 499 BC the then tyrant of Miletus, Aristagoras, launched a joint expedition with the Persian satrap <a href="/wiki/Artaphernes" title="Artaphernes">Artaphernes</a> to conquer Naxos, in an attempt to bolster his position in Miletus (both financially and in terms of prestige).<sup id="cite_ref-h153_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h153-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-V31_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-V31-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Naxos_(499_BC)" title="Siege of Naxos (499 BC)">mission</a> was a debacle,<sup id="cite_ref-V33_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-V33-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> and sensing his imminent removal as tyrant, Aristagoras chose to incite the whole of Ionia into rebellion against the Persian king <a href="/wiki/Darius_the_Great" title="Darius the Great">Darius the Great</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-h155_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h155-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ionian_Revolt_Campaign_Map.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Ionian_Revolt_Campaign_Map.png/200px-Ionian_Revolt_Campaign_Map.png" decoding="async" width="200" height="243" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Ionian_Revolt_Campaign_Map.png/300px-Ionian_Revolt_Campaign_Map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Ionian_Revolt_Campaign_Map.png/400px-Ionian_Revolt_Campaign_Map.png 2x" data-file-width="1645" data-file-height="2000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ionian_Revolt_Campaign_Map.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map showing main events of the Ionian Revolt.</div></div></div>
<p>Struggling to rule the independent-minded cities of Ionia, the Persians appointed local <a href="/wiki/Tyrant" title="Tyrant">tyrants</a> to rule each of them. This would prove to be the source of much trouble for the Greeks and Persians alike. In 498 BC, supported by troops from Athens and Eretria, the Ionians marched on, captured, and burnt Sardis.<sup id="cite_ref-V100*_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-V100*-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> However, on their return journey to Ionia, they were followed by Persian troops, and decisively beaten at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ephesus_(498_BC)" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Ephesus (498 BC)">Battle of Ephesus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-V102_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-V102-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> This campaign was the only offensive action taken by the Ionians, who subsequently went on the defensive. The Persians responded in 497 BC with a three-pronged attack aimed at recapturing the outlying areas of the rebellious territory,<sup id="cite_ref-V116_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-V116-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> but the spread of the revolt to Caria meant the largest army, under <a href="/wiki/Darius_the_Great" title="Darius the Great">Darius</a>, moved there instead.<sup id="cite_ref-V117_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-V117-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> While at first campaigning successfully in Caria, this army was wiped out in an ambush at the <a href="/wiki/Ionian_Revolt#Battle_of_Pedasus" title="Ionian Revolt">Battle of Pedasus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-V121_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-V121-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> This resulted in a stalemate for the rest of 496 and 495 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-board_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-board-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By 494 BC the Persian army and navy had regrouped, and they made straight for the epicentre of the rebellion at Miletus.<sup id="cite_ref-VI6_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VI6-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> The Ionian fleet sought to defend Miletus by sea, but was defeated decisively at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Lade" title="Battle of Lade">Battle of Lade</a>, after the <a href="/wiki/Samos" title="Samos">Samians</a> had defected.<sup id="cite_ref-VI8_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VI8-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Miletus was then besieged, captured, and its population was enslaved.<sup id="cite_ref-VI19_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VI19-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> This double defeat effectively ended the revolt, and the Carians surrendered to the Persians as a result.<sup id="cite_ref-VI25_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VI25-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> The Persians spent 493 BC reducing the cities along the west coast that still held out against them,<sup id="cite_ref-VI31_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VI31-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> before finally imposing a peace settlement on Ionia that was considered<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (June 2011)">by whom?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> to be both just and fair.<sup id="cite_ref-h175_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h175-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Ionian Revolt constituted the first major conflict between Greece and the Achaemenid Empire and represents the first phase of the Greco-Persian Wars. Asia Minor had been brought back into the Persian fold, but Darius had vowed to punish Athens and Eretria for their support for the revolt. Moreover, seeing that the political situation in Greece posed a continued threat to the stability of his Empire, he decided to embark on the conquest of all Greece.<sup id="cite_ref-h175_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h175-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="First_invasion_of_Greece_.28492.E2.80.93490_BC.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="First_invasion_of_Greece_(492–490_BC)">First invasion of Greece (492–490 BC)</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/First_Persian_invasion_of_Greece" title="First Persian invasion of Greece">First Persian invasion of Greece</a></div>
<div role="navigation" class="navbox" aria-labelledby="First_Persian_invasion_of_Greece" style="margin:0;float:right;clear:right;width:25.5em;margin-bottom:0.5em;margin-left:1em;;padding:3px"><table class="nowraplinks navbox-vertical mw-collapsible autocollapse navbox-inner" style="border-spacing:0;background:transparent;color:inherit"><tbody><tr><th scope="col" class="navbox-title" colspan="2" style="background-color:#C3D6EF;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Campaignbox_First_Persian_invasion_of_Greece" title="Template:Campaignbox First Persian invasion of Greece"><abbr title="View this template" style=";background-color:#C3D6EF;;background:none transparent;border:none;-moz-box-shadow:none;-webkit-box-shadow:none;box-shadow:none; padding:0;">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Campaignbox_First_Persian_invasion_of_Greece" title="Template talk:Campaignbox First Persian invasion of Greece"><abbr title="Discuss this template" style=";background-color:#C3D6EF;;background:none transparent;border:none;-moz-box-shadow:none;-webkit-box-shadow:none;box-shadow:none; padding:0;">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Campaignbox_First_Persian_invasion_of_Greece&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template" style=";background-color:#C3D6EF;;background:none transparent;border:none;-moz-box-shadow:none;-webkit-box-shadow:none;box-shadow:none; padding:0;">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div><div id="First_Persian_invasion_of_Greece" style="font-size:114%;margin:0 4em"><span style="line-height:1.6em"><a href="/wiki/First_Persian_invasion_of_Greece" title="First Persian invasion of Greece">First Persian invasion <br /> of Greece</a></span></div></th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="navbox-list navbox-odd hlist" style="width:100%;padding:0px"><div style="padding:0em 0.25em">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/First_Persian_invasion_of_Greece#Lindos" title="First Persian invasion of Greece">Lindos</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/First_Persian_invasion_of_Greece#Naxos" title="First Persian invasion of Greece">Naxos</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/First_Persian_invasion_of_Greece#Karystos" title="First Persian invasion of Greece">Karystos</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Eretria" title="Siege of Eretria">Eretria</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Marathon" title="Battle of Marathon">Marathon</a></li></ul>
</div></td></tr></tbody></table></div>
<p>After having reconquered Ionia, the Persians began to plan their next moves of extinguishing the threat to their empire from Greece; and punishing Athens and Eretria. The resultant <a href="/wiki/First_Persian_invasion_of_Greece" title="First Persian invasion of Greece">first Persian invasion of Greece</a> consisted of two main campaigns.<sup id="cite_ref-h177_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h177-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="492_BC:_Mardonius.27_campaign"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="492_BC:_Mardonius'_campaign">492 BC: Mardonius' campaign</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg/300px-Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg.png" decoding="async" width="300" height="240" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg/450px-Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg/600px-Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="992" data-file-height="793" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_Greco-Persian_Wars-en.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map showing events of the first phases of the Greco-Persian Wars</div></div></div>
<p>The first campaign, in 492 BC, was led by Darius's son-in-law <a href="/wiki/Mardonius_(general)" title="Mardonius (general)">Mardonius</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-VI43_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VI43-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> who re-subjugated <a href="/wiki/Thrace" title="Thrace">Thrace</a>, which had nominally been part of the Persian empire since 513 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Mardonius was also able to force <a href="/wiki/Macedon" class="mw-redirect" title="Macedon">Macedon</a> to become a fully subordinate client kingdom of Persia; it had previously been a <a href="/wiki/Vassal" title="Vassal">vassal</a>, but retained a broad degree of autonomy.<sup id="cite_ref-VI44_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VI44-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2011343_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2011343-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> However, further progress in this campaign was prevented when Mardonius's fleet was wrecked in a storm off the coast of <a href="/wiki/Mount_Athos" title="Mount Athos">Mount Athos</a>. Mardonius himself was then injured in a raid on his camp by a Thracian tribe, and after this he returned with the rest of the expedition to Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-VI44_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VI44-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-VI45_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VI45-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The following year, having given clear warning of his plans, Darius sent ambassadors to all the cities of Greece, demanding their submission. He received it from almost all of them, except Athens and <a href="/wiki/Sparta" title="Sparta">Sparta</a>, both of whom instead executed the ambassadors.<sup id="cite_ref-VI48_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VI48-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> With Athens still defiant, and Sparta now also effectively at war with him, Darius ordered a further military campaign for the following year.<sup id="cite_ref-h181_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h181-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="490_BC:_Datis_and_Artaphernes.27_campaign"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="490_BC:_Datis_and_Artaphernes'_campaign">490 BC: Datis and Artaphernes' campaign</span></h3>
<p>In 490 BC, <a href="/wiki/Datis" title="Datis">Datis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Artaphernes_(son_of_Artaphernes)" title="Artaphernes (son of Artaphernes)">Artaphernes</a> (son of the satrap <a href="/wiki/Artaphernes" title="Artaphernes">Artaphernes</a>) were given command of an amphibious invasion force, and set sail from <a href="/wiki/Cilicia" title="Cilicia">Cilicia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-h181_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h181-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>  The Persian force sailed first to the island of <a href="/wiki/Rhodes" title="Rhodes">Rhodes</a>, where a Lindian Temple Chronicle records that <a href="/wiki/Datis" title="Datis">Datis</a> besieged the city of <a href="/wiki/Lindos" title="Lindos">Lindos</a>, but was unsuccessful.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> The fleet sailed next to Naxos, to punish the Naxians for their resistance to the failed expedition the Persians had mounted there a decade earlier.<sup id="cite_ref-h183_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h183-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Many of the inhabitants fled to the mountains; those that the Persians caught were enslaved. The Persians then burnt the city and temples of the Naxians.<sup id="cite_ref-VI96_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VI96-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> The fleet then proceeded to island-hop across the rest of the Aegean on its way to Eretria, taking hostages and troops from each island.<sup id="cite_ref-h183_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h183-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The task force sailed on to <a href="/wiki/Euboea" title="Euboea">Euboea</a>, and to the first major target, Eretria.<sup id="cite_ref-VI100_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VI100-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> The Eretrians made no attempt to stop the Persians from landing or advancing and thus allowed themselves to be <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Eretria" title="Siege of Eretria">besieged</a>. For six days, the Persians attacked the walls, with losses on both sides; however, on the seventh day two reputable Eretrians opened the gates and betrayed the city to the Persians. The city was razed, and temples and shrines were looted and burned. Furthermore, according to Darius's commands, the Persians enslaved all the remaining townspeople.<sup id="cite_ref-VI101_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VI101-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Battle_of_Marathon">Battle of Marathon</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Marathon" title="Battle of Marathon">Battle of Marathon</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Marathon_Greek_Double_Envelopment.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Battle_of_Marathon_Greek_Double_Envelopment.png/250px-Battle_of_Marathon_Greek_Double_Envelopment.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="197" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Battle_of_Marathon_Greek_Double_Envelopment.png/375px-Battle_of_Marathon_Greek_Double_Envelopment.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Battle_of_Marathon_Greek_Double_Envelopment.png/500px-Battle_of_Marathon_Greek_Double_Envelopment.png 2x" data-file-width="704" data-file-height="554" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Marathon_Greek_Double_Envelopment.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Greek wings envelop the Persians</div></div></div>
<p>The Persian fleet next headed south down the coast of <a href="/wiki/Attica" title="Attica">Attica</a>, landing at the bay of <a href="/wiki/Marathon,_Greece" title="Marathon, Greece">Marathon</a>, roughly 40 kilometres (25&#160;mi) from Athens.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Under the guidance of <a href="/wiki/Miltiades_the_Younger" class="mw-redirect" title="Miltiades the Younger">Miltiades</a>, the general with the greatest experience of fighting the Persians, the Athenian army marched to block the two exits from the plain of Marathon. Stalemate ensued for five days, before the Persians  decided to continue onward to Athens, and began to load their troops back onto the ships.  After the Persians had loaded their cavalry (their strongest soldiers) on the ships, the 10,000 Athenian soldiers descended from the hills around the plain.  The Greeks crushed the weaker Persian foot soldiers by routing the wings before turning towards the centre of the Persian line. The remnants of the Persian army fled to their ships and left the battle.<sup id="cite_ref-h195_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h195-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Herodotus records that 6,400 Persian bodies were counted on the battlefield; the Athenians lost only 192 men.<sup id="cite_ref-VI117_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VI117-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As soon as the Persian survivors had put to sea, the Athenians marched as quickly as possible to Athens.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> They arrived in time to prevent Artaphernes from securing a landing in Athens. Seeing his opportunity lost, Artaphernes ended the year's campaign and returned to Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Battle of Marathon was a watershed in the Greco-Persian wars, showing the Greeks that the Persians could be beaten. It also highlighted the superiority of the more heavily armoured Greek hoplites, and showed their potential when used wisely.<sup id="cite_ref-h195_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h195-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Interbellum_.28490.E2.80.93480_BC.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Interbellum_(490–480_BC)">Interbellum (490–480 BC)</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Achaemenid_Empire">Achaemenid Empire</span></h3>
<p>After the failure of the first invasion, Darius began raising a huge new army with which he intended to subjugate Greece completely. However, in 486 BC, his <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egyptian</a> subjects revolted, and the revolt forced an indefinite postponement of any Greek expedition.<sup id="cite_ref-h202_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h202-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Darius died while preparing to march on Egypt, and the throne of Persia passed to his son <a href="/wiki/Xerxes_I" title="Xerxes I">Xerxes I</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Xerxes crushed the Egyptian revolt, and very quickly resumed the preparations for the invasion of Greece.<sup id="cite_ref-h208_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h208-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Since this was to be a full-scale invasion, it needed longterm planning, stockpiling and conscription. Xerxes decided that the <a href="/wiki/Hellespont" class="mw-redirect" title="Hellespont">Hellespont</a> would be bridged to allow his army to cross to Europe, and that a canal should be dug across the isthmus of <a href="/wiki/Mount_Athos" title="Mount Athos">Mount Athos</a> (a Persian fleet had been destroyed in 492 BC while rounding this coastline). These were both feats of exceptional ambition that would have been beyond the capabilities of any other contemporary state.<sup id="cite_ref-h213_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h213-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> However, the campaign was delayed by one year because of another revolt in Egypt and <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Persians had the sympathy of several Greek city-states, including <a href="/wiki/Argos" title="Argos">Argos</a>, which had pledged to defect when the Persians reached their borders.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Aleuadae" title="Aleuadae">Aleuadae</a> family, who ruled <a href="/wiki/Larissa" title="Larissa">Larissa</a> in <a href="/wiki/Thessaly" title="Thessaly">Thessaly</a>, saw the invasion as an opportunity to extend their power.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thebes,_Greece" title="Thebes, Greece">Thebes</a>, though not explicitly 'Medising', was suspected of being willing to aid the Persians once the invasion force arrived.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-h263_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h263-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 481 BC, after roughly four years of preparation, Xerxes began to muster the troops to invade Europe. Herodotus gives the names of 46 nations from which troops were drafted.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> The Persian army was gathered in Asia Minor in the summer and autumn of 481 BC. The armies from the Eastern satrapies were gathered in <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kritala&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Kritala (page does not exist)">Kritala</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cappadocia" title="Cappadocia">Cappadocia</a> and were led by Xerxes to Sardis where they passed the winter.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Early in spring, it moved to <a href="/wiki/Abydos,_Hellespont" class="mw-redirect" title="Abydos, Hellespont">Abydos</a> where it was joined with the armies of the western satrapies.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> Then the army that Xerxes had mustered marched towards Europe, crossing the Hellespont on two <a href="/wiki/Pontoon_bridge" title="Pontoon bridge">pontoon bridges</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Size_of_the_Persian_forces">Size of the Persian forces</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece#Size_of_the_Persian_forces" title="Second Persian invasion of Greece">Second Persian invasion of Greece §&#160;Size of the Persian forces</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Xerxes_all_ethnicities.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Xerxes_all_ethnicities.jpg/400px-Xerxes_all_ethnicities.jpg" decoding="async" width="400" height="196" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Xerxes_all_ethnicities.jpg/600px-Xerxes_all_ethnicities.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Xerxes_all_ethnicities.jpg/800px-Xerxes_all_ethnicities.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4770" data-file-height="2335" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Xerxes_all_ethnicities.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The soldiers of <a href="/wiki/Xerxes_I" title="Xerxes I">Xerxes I</a>, of all ethnicities,<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> on the tomb of Xerxes I, at <a href="/wiki/Naqsh-e_Rostam" title="Naqsh-e Rostam">Naqsh-e Rostam</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The numbers of troops that Xerxes mustered for the second invasion of Greece have been the subject of endless dispute. Most modern scholars reject as unrealistic the figures of 2.5 million given by Herodotus and other ancient sources because the victors likely miscalculated or exaggerated. The topic has been hotly debated, but the consensus revolves around the figure of 200,000.<sup id="cite_ref-souza_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-souza-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The size of the Persian fleet is also disputed, although perhaps less so. Other ancient authors agree with Herodotus' number of 1,207. These numbers are by ancient standards consistent, and this could be interpreted that a number around 1,200 is correct. Among modern scholars, some have accepted this number, although suggesting the number must have been lower by the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Salamis" title="Battle of Salamis">Battle of Salamis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-L93_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-L93-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> Other recent works on the Persian Wars reject this number, viewing 1,207 as more of a reference to the combined Greek fleet in the <a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a>. These works generally claim that the Persians could have launched no more than around 600 warships into the Aegean.<sup id="cite_ref-L93_112-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-L93-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Greek_city_states">Greek city states</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Athens">Athens</span></h4>
<p>A year after Marathon, Miltiades, the hero of Marathon, was injured in a military campaign to <a href="/wiki/Paros" title="Paros">Paros</a>. Taking advantage of his incapacitation, the powerful <a href="/wiki/Alcmaeonid" class="mw-redirect" title="Alcmaeonid">Alcmaeonid</a> family arranged for him to be prosecuted for the failure of the campaign. A huge fine was imposed on Miltiades for the crime of 'deceiving the Athenian people', but he died weeks later from his wound.<sup id="cite_ref-h214_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h214-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The politician <a href="/wiki/Themistocles" title="Themistocles">Themistocles</a>, with a power base firmly established amongst the poor, filled the vacuum left by Miltiades's death, and in the following decade became the most influential politician in Athens. During this period, Themistocles continued to support the expansion of Athens' naval power.<sup id="cite_ref-h214_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h214-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> The Athenians were aware throughout this period that the Persian interest in Greece had not ended,<sup id="cite_ref-h208_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h208-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> and Themistocles's naval policies may be seen in the light of the potential threat from Persia.<sup id="cite_ref-h214_115-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h214-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Aristides, Themistocles's great rival, and champion of the <i>zeugites</i> (the 'upper hoplite-class') vigorously opposed such a policy.<sup id="cite_ref-h217_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h217-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 483&#160;BC, a vast new seam of silver was found in the Athenian mines at <a href="/wiki/Laurium" title="Laurium">Laurium</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-PT4_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PT4-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> Themistocles proposed that the silver should be used to build a new fleet of triremes, ostensibly to assist in a long running war with <a href="/wiki/Aegina" title="Aegina">Aegina</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-h219_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h219-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> Plutarch suggests that Themistocles deliberately avoided mentioning Persia, believing that it was too distant a threat for the Athenians to act on, but that countering Persia was the fleet's aim.<sup id="cite_ref-PT4_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PT4-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> Fine suggests that many Athenians must have admitted that such a fleet would be needed to resist the Persians, whose preparations for the coming campaign were known. Themistocles's motion was passed easily, despite strong opposition from Aristides. Its passage was probably due to the desire of many of the poorer Athenians for paid employment as rowers in the fleet.<sup id="cite_ref-fine292_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fine292-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> It is unclear from the ancient sources whether 100 or 200 ships were initially authorised; both Fine and Holland suggest that at first 100 ships were authorised and that a second vote increased this number to the levels seen during the second invasion.<sup id="cite_ref-h219_118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h219-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-fine292_119-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fine292-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> Aristides continued to oppose Themistocles's policy, and tension between the two camps built over the winter, so the <a href="/wiki/Ostracism" title="Ostracism">ostracism</a> of 482&#160;BC became a direct contest between Themistocles and Aristides. In what Holland characterises as, in essence, the world's first referendum, Aristides was ostracised, and Themistocles's policies were endorsed. Indeed, becoming aware of the Persian preparations for the coming invasion, the Athenians voted to build more ships than those for which Themistocles had asked.<sup id="cite_ref-h219_118-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h219-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>  Thus, during the preparations for the Persian invasion, Themistocles had become the leading politician in Athens.<sup id="cite_ref-PT5_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PT5-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sparta">Sparta</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Vix_crater_hoplite_circa_500_BCE.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Vix_crater_hoplite_circa_500_BCE.jpg/180px-Vix_crater_hoplite_circa_500_BCE.jpg" decoding="async" width="180" height="287" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Vix_crater_hoplite_circa_500_BCE.jpg/270px-Vix_crater_hoplite_circa_500_BCE.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Vix_crater_hoplite_circa_500_BCE.jpg/360px-Vix_crater_hoplite_circa_500_BCE.jpg 2x" data-file-width="603" data-file-height="962" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Vix_crater_hoplite_circa_500_BCE.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Probable <a href="/wiki/Spartan" class="mw-redirect" title="Spartan">Spartan</a> hoplite (<a href="/wiki/Vix_crater" class="mw-redirect" title="Vix crater">Vix crater</a>, c. 500 BC).<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The Spartan king <a href="/wiki/Demaratus" title="Demaratus">Demaratus</a> had been stripped of his kingship in 491 BC, and replaced with his cousin <a href="/wiki/Leotychides" class="mw-redirect" title="Leotychides">Leotychides</a>. Sometime after 490 BC, the humiliated Demaratus had chosen to go into exile, and had made his way to Darius's court in <a href="/wiki/Susa" title="Susa">Susa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-h202_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h202-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Demaratus would from then on act as an advisor to Darius, and later Xerxes, on Greek affairs, and accompanied Xerxes during the second Persian invasion.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> At the end of Herodotus's book 7, there is an anecdote relating that prior to the second invasion, Demaratus sent an apparently blank wax tablet to Sparta. When the wax was removed, a message was found scratched on the wooden backing, warning the Spartans of Xerxes's plans.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> However, many historians believe that this chapter was inserted into the text by a later author, possibly to fill a <a href="/wiki/Lacuna_(manuscripts)" title="Lacuna (manuscripts)">gap</a> between the end of book 7 and the start of book 8. The veracity of this anecdote is therefore unclear.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hellenic_alliance">Hellenic alliance</span></h4>
<p>In 481 BC, Xerxes sent ambassadors to city states throughout Greece, asking for food, land, and water as tokens of their submission to Persia.  However, Xerxes' ambassadors deliberately avoided Athens and Sparta, hoping thereby that those states would not learn of the Persians' plans.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> States that were opposed to Persia thus began to coalesce around these two city states. A congress of states met at <a href="/wiki/Corinth" title="Corinth">Corinth</a> in late autumn of 481 BC, and a confederate alliance of <a href="/wiki/History_of_Greece" title="History of Greece">Greek city-states</a> was formed.<sup id="cite_ref-VII145_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VII145-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> This confederation had powers both to send envoys to ask for assistance and to dispatch troops from the member states to defensive points after joint consultation. Herodotus does not formulate an abstract name for the union but simply calls them "οἱ  Ἕλληνες" (the Greeks) and "the Greeks who had sworn alliance" (Godley translation) or "the Greeks who had banded themselves together" (Rawlinson translation).<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> From now on, they will be referred to as the 'Allies'. Sparta and Athens had a leading role in the congress but the interests of all the states influenced defensive strategy.<sup id="cite_ref-VII160_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VII160-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> Little is known about the internal workings of the congress or the discussions during its meetings. Only 70 of the nearly 700 Greek city-states sent representatives. Nevertheless, this was remarkable for the disjointed Greek world, especially since many of the city-states present were still technically at war with one another.<sup id="cite_ref-h226_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h226-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Second_invasion_of_Greece_.28480.E2.80.93479_BC.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_invasion_of_Greece_(480–479_BC)">Second invasion of Greece (480–479 BC)</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece" title="Second Persian invasion of Greece">Second Persian invasion of Greece</a></div>
<div role="navigation" class="navbox" aria-labelledby="Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece" style="margin:0;float:right;clear:right;width:25.5em;margin-bottom:0.5em;margin-left:1em;;padding:3px"><table class="nowraplinks navbox-vertical mw-collapsible autocollapse navbox-inner" style="border-spacing:0;background:transparent;color:inherit"><tbody><tr><th scope="col" class="navbox-title" colspan="2" style="background-color:#C3D6EF;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Campaignbox_Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece" title="Template:Campaignbox Second Persian invasion of Greece"><abbr title="View this template" style=";background-color:#C3D6EF;;background:none transparent;border:none;-moz-box-shadow:none;-webkit-box-shadow:none;box-shadow:none; padding:0;">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Campaignbox_Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece" title="Template talk:Campaignbox Second Persian invasion of Greece"><abbr title="Discuss this template" style=";background-color:#C3D6EF;;background:none transparent;border:none;-moz-box-shadow:none;-webkit-box-shadow:none;box-shadow:none; padding:0;">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Campaignbox_Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template" style=";background-color:#C3D6EF;;background:none transparent;border:none;-moz-box-shadow:none;-webkit-box-shadow:none;box-shadow:none; padding:0;">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div><div id="Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece" style="font-size:114%;margin:0 4em"><span style="line-height:1.6em"><a href="/wiki/Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece" title="Second Persian invasion of Greece">Second Persian <br /> invasion of Greece</a></span></div></th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="navbox-list navbox-odd hlist" style="width:100%;padding:0px"><div style="padding:0em 0.25em">
