</div></td></tr></tbody></table></div>
<h3><span id="Early_480_BC:_Thrace.2C_Macedonia.2C_and_Thessaly"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_480_BC:_Thrace,_Macedonia,_and_Thessaly">Early 480 BC: Thrace, Macedonia, and Thessaly</span></h3>
<p>Having crossed into Europe in April 480 BC, the Persian army began its march to Greece, taking 3 months to travel unopposed from the Hellespont to <a href="/wiki/Therme" class="mw-redirect" title="Therme">Therme</a>. It paused at <a href="/wiki/Doriskos" class="mw-redirect" title="Doriskos">Doriskos</a> where it was joined by the fleet. Xerxes reorganized the troops into tactical units replacing the national formations used earlier for the march.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Thermopylae_and_movements_to_Salamis,_480_BC.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Battle_of_Thermopylae_and_movements_to_Salamis%2C_480_BC.gif/300px-Battle_of_Thermopylae_and_movements_to_Salamis%2C_480_BC.gif" decoding="async" width="300" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Battle_of_Thermopylae_and_movements_to_Salamis%2C_480_BC.gif/450px-Battle_of_Thermopylae_and_movements_to_Salamis%2C_480_BC.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Battle_of_Thermopylae_and_movements_to_Salamis%2C_480_BC.gif/600px-Battle_of_Thermopylae_and_movements_to_Salamis%2C_480_BC.gif 2x" data-file-width="747" data-file-height="561" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Thermopylae_and_movements_to_Salamis,_480_BC.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Major events in the second invasion of Greece</div></div></div>
<p>The Allied 'congress' met again in the spring of 480 BC and agreed to defend the narrow <a href="/wiki/Vale_of_Tempe" title="Vale of Tempe">Vale of Tempe</a> on the borders of Thessaly and block Xerxes's advance.<sup id="cite_ref-h248_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h248-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> However, once there, they were warned by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_I_of_Macedon" title="Alexander I of Macedon">Alexander I of Macedon</a> that the vale could be bypassed and that the army of Xerxes was overwhelmingly large, thus the Greeks retreated. Shortly afterwards, they received the news that Xerxes had crossed the Hellespont.<sup id="cite_ref-VII173_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VII173-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> At this point, a second strategy was suggested by Themistocles to the allies. The route to southern Greece (<a href="/wiki/Boeotia" title="Boeotia">Boeotia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Attica" title="Attica">Attica</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Peloponnesus" class="mw-redirect" title="Peloponnesus">Peloponnesus</a>) would require the army of Xerxes to travel through the narrow pass of <a href="/wiki/Thermopylae" title="Thermopylae">Thermopylae</a>. This could easily be blocked by the Greek hoplites, despite the overwhelming numbers of Persians. Furthermore, to prevent the Persians bypassing Thermopylae by sea, the Athenian and allied navies could block the straits of <a href="/wiki/Artemisium" title="Artemisium">Artemisium</a>. This dual strategy was adopted by the congress.<sup id="cite_ref-h255_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h255-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> However, the Peloponnesian cities made fall-back plans to defend the <a href="/wiki/Isthmus_of_Corinth" title="Isthmus of Corinth">Isthmus of Corinth</a> should it come to it, while the women and children of Athens were evacuated to the Peloponnesian city of <a href="/wiki/Troezen" title="Troezen">Troezen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="August_480_BC:_Battles_of_Thermopylae_and_Artemisium">August 480 BC: Battles of Thermopylae and Artemisium</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Thermopylae" title="Battle of Thermopylae">Battle of Thermopylae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Artemisium" title="Battle of Artemisium">Battle of Artemisium</a></div>
<p>Xerxes's estimated time of arrival at Thermopylae coincided with both the Olympic Games and the festival of <a href="/wiki/Carneia" class="mw-redirect" title="Carneia">Carneia</a>. For the Spartans, warfare during these periods was considered sacrilegious. Despite the uncomfortable timing, the Spartans considered the threat so grave that they dispatched their king <a href="/wiki/Leonidas_I" title="Leonidas I">Leonidas I</a> with his personal bodyguard (the <i>Hippeis</i>) of 300 men. The customary elite young men in the Hippeis were replaced by veterans who already had children. Leonidas was supported by contingents from the Allied Peloponnesian cities, and other forces that the Allies picked up on the way to Thermopylae.<sup id="cite_ref-h257_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h257-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> The Allies proceeded to occupy the pass, rebuilt the wall the <a href="/wiki/Phocis_(ancient_region)" title="Phocis (ancient region)">Phocians</a> had built at the narrowest point of the pass, and waited for Xerxes's arrival.<sup id="cite_ref-h262_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h262-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thermopylae_ancient_coastline_large.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Thermopylae_ancient_coastline_large.jpg/300px-Thermopylae_ancient_coastline_large.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Thermopylae_ancient_coastline_large.jpg/450px-Thermopylae_ancient_coastline_large.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Thermopylae_ancient_coastline_large.jpg/600px-Thermopylae_ancient_coastline_large.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thermopylae_ancient_coastline_large.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The pass of Thermopylae</div></div></div>
<p>When the Persians arrived at Thermopylae in mid-August, they initially waited for three days for the Allies to disperse. When Xerxes was eventually persuaded that the Allies intended to contest the pass, he sent his troops to attack.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> However, the Allied position was ideally suited to <a href="/wiki/Hoplite" title="Hoplite">hoplite</a> warfare, the Persian contingents being forced to attack the Greek <a href="/wiki/Phalanx_formation" class="mw-redirect" title="Phalanx formation">phalanx</a> head on.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> The Allies withstood two full days of Persian attacks, including those by the elite <a href="/wiki/Persian_Immortals" class="mw-redirect" title="Persian Immortals">Persian Immortals</a>. However, towards the end of the second day, they were betrayed by a local resident named <a href="/wiki/Ephialtes_of_Trachis" title="Ephialtes of Trachis">Ephialtes</a> who revealed to Xerxes a mountain path that led behind the Allied lines. Made aware by scouts that they were being outflanked, Leonidas dismissed most of the Allied army, remaining to guard the rear with perhaps 2,000 men. On the final day of the battle, the remaining Allies sallied forth from the wall to meet the Persians in the wider part of the pass to slaughter as many Persians as they could, but eventually they were all killed or captured.<sup id="cite_ref-VII223_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VII223-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Simultaneous with the battle at Thermopylae, an Allied naval force of 271 triremes defended the Straits of <a href="/wiki/Artemisium" title="Artemisium">Artemisium</a> against the Persians, thus protecting the flank of the forces at Thermopylae.<sup id="cite_ref-VIII2_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VIII2-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> Here the Allied fleet held off the Persians for three days; however, on the third evening the Allies received news of the fate of Leonidas and the Allied troops at Thermopylae. Since the Allied fleet was badly damaged, and since it no longer needed to defend the flank of Thermopylae, the Allies retreated from Artemisium to the island of <a href="/wiki/Salamis_Island" title="Salamis Island">Salamis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<p><span id="Sack_of_Athens"></span><span id="Sack_of_Athens_(480_BC)"></span><span id="Siege_of_the_Acropolis"></span><span id="Siege_of_the_Acropolis_(480_BC)"></span>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="September_480_BC:_Battle_of_Salamis">September 480 BC: Battle of Salamis</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Destruction_of_Athens" class="mw-redirect" title="Destruction of Athens">Destruction of Athens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Salamis" title="Battle of Salamis">Battle of Salamis</a></div>
<p>Victory at Thermopylae meant that all <a href="/wiki/Boeotia" title="Boeotia">Boeotia</a> fell to Xerxes; Attica was then open to invasion. The remaining population of Athens was evacuated, with the aid of the Allied fleet, to Salamis.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> The Peloponnesian Allies began to prepare a defensive line across the <a href="/wiki/Isthmus_of_Corinth" title="Isthmus of Corinth">Isthmus of Corinth</a>, building a wall, and demolishing the road from <a href="/wiki/Megara" title="Megara">Megara</a>, abandoning Athens to the Persians.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> Athens thus fell to the Persians; the small number of Athenians who had barricaded themselves on the <a href="/wiki/Acropolis" title="Acropolis">Acropolis</a> were eventually defeated, and Xerxes then ordered the <a href="/wiki/Destruction_of_Athens" class="mw-redirect" title="Destruction of Athens">Destruction of Athens</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_salamis.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Battle_of_salamis.png/300px-Battle_of_salamis.png" decoding="async" width="300" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Battle_of_salamis.png/450px-Battle_of_salamis.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Battle_of_salamis.png/600px-Battle_of_salamis.png 2x" data-file-width="741" data-file-height="556" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_salamis.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Schematic diagram illustrating events during the Battle of Salamis</div></div></div>
<p>The Persians had now captured most of Greece, but Xerxes had perhaps not expected such defiance; his priority was now to complete the war as quickly as possible.<sup id="cite_ref-h327_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h327-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> If Xerxes could destroy the Allied navy, he would be in a strong position to force an Allied surrender;<sup id="cite_ref-h308_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h308-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> conversely by avoiding destruction, or as Themistocles hoped, by destroying the Persian fleet, the Allies could prevent conquest from being completed.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> The Allied fleet thus remained off the coast of Salamis into September, despite the imminent arrival of the Persians. Even after Athens fell, the Allied fleet remained off the coast of Salamis, trying to lure the Persian fleet to battle.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> Partly because of deception by Themistocles, the navies met in the cramped Straits of Salamis.<sup id="cite_ref-h310_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h310-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> There, the Persian numbers became a hindrance, as ships struggled to maneuver and became disorganised.<sup id="cite_ref-VIII89_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VIII89-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> Seizing the opportunity, the Allied fleet attacked, and scored a decisive victory, sinking or capturing at least 200 Persian ships, therefore ensuring the safety of the Peloponnessus.<sup id="cite_ref-h320_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h320-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Herodotus, after the loss of the battle Xerxes attempted to build a causeway across the channel to attack the Athenian evacuees on Salamis, but this project was soon abandoned. With the Persians' naval superiority removed, Xerxes feared that the Allies might sail to the Hellespont and destroy the pontoon bridges.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> His general Mardonius volunteered to remain in Greece and complete the conquest with a hand-picked group of troops, while Xerxes retreated to Asia with the bulk of the army.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> Mardonius over-wintered in Boeotia and Thessaly; the Athenians were thus able to return to their burnt-out city for the winter.<sup id="cite_ref-h327_145-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h327-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="June_479_BC:_Battles_of_Plataea_and_Mycale">June 479 BC: Battles of Plataea and Mycale</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Plataea" title="Battle of Plataea">Battle of Plataea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Mycale" title="Battle of Mycale">Battle of Mycale</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Spartans_at_Plataea.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Spartans_at_Plataea.jpg/220px-Spartans_at_Plataea.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Spartans_at_Plataea.jpg/330px-Spartans_at_Plataea.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Spartans_at_Plataea.jpg/440px-Spartans_at_Plataea.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1622" data-file-height="1106" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Spartans_at_Plataea.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Spartans fighting against Persian forces at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Plataea" title="Battle of Plataea">Battle of Plataea</a>. 19th century illustration.</div></div></div>
<p>Over the winter, there was some tension among the Allies. In particular, the Athenians, who were not protected by the Isthmus, but whose fleet was the key to the security of the Peloponnesus, felt that they had been treated unfairly, and so they refused to join the Allied navy in the spring.<sup id="cite_ref-h333_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h333-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mardonius_(general)" title="Mardonius (general)">Mardonius</a> remained in Thessaly, knowing an attack on the Isthmus was pointless, while the Allies refused to send an army outside the Peloponessus.<sup id="cite_ref-h333_154-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h333-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> Mardonius moved to break the stalemate, by offering peace to the Athenians, using <a href="/wiki/Alexander_I_of_Macedon" title="Alexander I of Macedon">Alexander I of Macedon</a> as an intermediate. The Athenians made sure that a Spartan delegation was on hand to hear the Athenians reject the Persians' offer.<sup id="cite_ref-h336_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h336-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> Athens was thus evacuated again, and the Persians marched south and re-took possession of it. Mardonius now repeated his offer of peace to the Athenian refugees on Salamis. Athens, with <a href="/wiki/Megara" title="Megara">Megara</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plataea" title="Plataea">Plataea</a>, sent emissaries to Sparta demanding assistance, and threatening to accept the Persian terms if they were not aided.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> In response, the Spartans summoned a large army from the Peloponnese cities and marched to meet the Persians.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>When Mardonius heard the Allied army was on the march, he retreated into Boeotia, near <a href="/wiki/Plataea" title="Plataea">Plataea</a>, trying to draw the Allies into open terrain where he could use his cavalry.<sup id="cite_ref-h339_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h339-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> The Allied army, under the command of the regent <a href="/wiki/Pausanias_(general)" title="Pausanias (general)">Pausanias</a>, stayed on high ground above Plataea to protect themselves against such tactics. After several days of maneuver and stalemate, Pausanias ordered a night-time retreat towards the Allies' original positions. This maneuver went awry, leaving the Athenians, and Spartans and Tegeans isolated on separate hills, with the other contingents scattered further away near Plataea.<sup id="cite_ref-h342_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h342-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> Seeing that the Persians might never have a better opportunity to attack, Mardonius ordered his whole army forward.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> However, the Persian infantry proved no match for the heavily armoured Greek hoplites,<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> and the Spartans broke through to Mardonius's bodyguard and killed him.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> After this the Persian force dissolved in rout; 40,000 troops managed to escape via the road to Thessaly,<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> but the rest fled to the Persian camp where they were trapped and slaughtered by the Greeks, finalising the Greek victory.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-h350_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h350-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Herodotus recounts that, on the afternoon of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Plataea" title="Battle of Plataea">Battle of Plataea</a>, a rumour of their victory at that battle reached the Allies' navy, at that time off the coast of <a href="/wiki/Mycale" title="Mycale">Mount Mycale</a> in Ionia.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> Their morale boosted, the Allied marines fought and won a decisive victory at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Mycale" title="Battle of Mycale">Battle of Mycale</a> that same day, destroying the remnants of the Persian fleet, crippling Xerxes's sea power, and marking the ascendancy of the Greek fleet.<sup id="cite_ref-h357_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h357-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> Whilst many modern historians doubt that Mycale took place on the same day as Plataea, the battle may well only have occurred once the Allies received news of the events unfolding in Greece.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Greek_counterattack_.28479.E2.80.93478_BC.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Greek_counterattack_(479–478_BC)">Greek counterattack (479–478 BC)</span></h2>
<div role="navigation" class="navbox" aria-labelledby="Greek_counterattack" style="margin:0;float:right;clear:right;width:25.5em;margin-bottom:0.5em;margin-left:1em;;padding:3px"><table class="nowraplinks navbox-vertical mw-collapsible autocollapse navbox-inner" style="border-spacing:0;background:transparent;color:inherit"><tbody><tr><th scope="col" class="navbox-title" colspan="2" style="background-color:#C3D6EF;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Campaignbox_Greek_counterattack" title="Template:Campaignbox Greek counterattack"><abbr title="View this template" style=";background-color:#C3D6EF;;background:none transparent;border:none;-moz-box-shadow:none;-webkit-box-shadow:none;box-shadow:none; padding:0;">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Campaignbox_Greek_counterattack" title="Template talk:Campaignbox Greek counterattack"><abbr title="Discuss this template" style=";background-color:#C3D6EF;;background:none transparent;border:none;-moz-box-shadow:none;-webkit-box-shadow:none;box-shadow:none; padding:0;">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Campaignbox_Greek_counterattack&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template" style=";background-color:#C3D6EF;;background:none transparent;border:none;-moz-box-shadow:none;-webkit-box-shadow:none;box-shadow:none; padding:0;">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div><div id="Greek_counterattack" style="font-size:114%;margin:0 4em"><span style="line-height:1.6em"><a href="/wiki/Greco%E2%80%93Persian_Wars#Greek_counterattack_(479–478_BC)" class="mw-redirect" title="Greco–Persian Wars"><span class="wrap">Greek counterattack</span></a></span></div></th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="navbox-list navbox-odd hlist" style="width:100%;padding:0px"><div style="padding:0em 0.25em">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Mycale" title="Battle of Mycale">Mycale</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Greco-Persian_Wars#Sestos" title="Greco-Persian Wars">Sestos</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Greco%E2%80%93Persian_Wars#Cyprus" class="mw-redirect" title="Greco–Persian Wars">Cyprus</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Greco%E2%80%93Persian_Wars#Byzantium" class="mw-redirect" title="Greco–Persian Wars">Byzantium</a></li></ul>
</div></td></tr></tbody></table></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mycale_and_Ionia">Mycale and Ionia</span></h3>
<p>Mycale was, in many ways, the beginning of a new phase in the conflict, in which the Greeks would go on the offensive against the Persians.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> The immediate result of the victory at Mycale was a second revolt amongst the Greek cities of Asia Minor. The Samians and Milesians had actively fought against the Persians at Mycale, thus openly declaring their rebellion, and the other cities followed in their example.<sup id="cite_ref-IX104_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IX104-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TI89_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TI89-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sestos">Sestos</span></h3>
<p>Shortly after Mycale, the Allied fleet sailed to the Hellespont to break down the pontoon bridges, but found that this had already been done.<sup id="cite_ref-IX114_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IX114-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> The Peloponnesians sailed home, but the Athenians remained to attack the <a href="/wiki/Thracian_Chersonese" class="mw-redirect" title="Thracian Chersonese">Chersonesos</a>, still held by the Persians.<sup id="cite_ref-IX114_172-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IX114-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> The Persians and their allies made for <a href="/wiki/Sestos" title="Sestos">Sestos</a>, the strongest town in the region. Amongst them was one <a href="/w/index.php?title=Oeobazus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Oeobazus (page does not exist)">Oeobazus</a> of <a href="/wiki/Cardia_(Thrace)" title="Cardia (Thrace)">Cardia</a>, who had with him the cables and other equipment from the pontoon bridges.<sup id="cite_ref-IX115_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IX115-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> The Persian governor, <a href="/wiki/Artayctes" title="Artayctes">Artayctes</a> had not prepared for a siege, not believing that the Allies would attack.<sup id="cite_ref-IX116_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IX116-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> The Athenians therefore were able to lay a siege around Sestos.<sup id="cite_ref-IX114_172-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IX114-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> The siege dragged on for several months, causing some discontent amongst the Athenian troops,<sup id="cite_ref-IX117_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IX117-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> but eventually, when the food ran out in the city, the Persians fled at night from the least guarded area of the city. The Athenians were thus able to take possession of the city the next day.<sup id="cite_ref-IX118_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IX118-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Most of the Athenian troops were sent straight away to pursue the Persians.<sup id="cite_ref-IX118_176-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IX118-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> The party of Oeobazus was captured by a Thracian tribe, and Oeobazus was sacrificed to the god <a href="/w/index.php?title=Plistorus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Plistorus (page does not exist)">Plistorus</a>. The Athenians eventually caught Artayctes, killing some of the Persians with him but taking most of them, including Artayctes, captive.<sup id="cite_ref-IX119_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IX119-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> Artayctes was crucified at the request of the people of <a href="/wiki/Elaeus" title="Elaeus">Elaeus</a>, a town which Artayctes had plundered while governor of the Chersonesos.<sup id="cite_ref-IX120_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IX120-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> The Athenians, having pacified the region, then sailed back to Athens, taking the cables from the pontoon bridges with them as trophies.<sup id="cite_ref-IX121_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IX121-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cyprus">Cyprus</span></h3>
<p>In 478 BC, still operating under the terms of the Hellenic alliance, the Allies sent out a fleet composed of 20 Peloponnesian and 30 Athenian ships supported by an unspecified number of allies, under the overall command of Pausanias. According to Thucydides, this fleet sailed to Cyprus and "subdued most of the island".<sup id="cite_ref-TI94_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TI94-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> Exactly what Thucydides means by this is unclear. Sealey suggests that this was essentially a raid to gather as much treasure as possible from the Persian garrisons on Cyprus.<sup id="cite_ref-Sea242_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sea242-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> There is no indication that the Allies attempted to take possession of the island, and, shortly after, they sailed to Byzantium.<sup id="cite_ref-TI94_180-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TI94-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> Certainly, the fact that the Delian League repeatedly campaigned in Cyprus suggests either that the island was not garrisoned by the Allies in 478 BC, or that the garrisons were quickly expelled.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Byzantium">Byzantium</span></h3>
<p>The Greek fleet then sailed to <a href="/wiki/Byzantium" title="Byzantium">Byzantium</a>, which they besieged and eventually captured.<sup id="cite_ref-TI94_180-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TI94-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> Control of both Sestos and Byzantium gave the allies command of the straits between Europe and Asia (over which the Persians had crossed), and allowed them access to the merchant trade of the Black Sea.<sup id="cite_ref-Fine331_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fine331-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The aftermath of the siege was to prove troublesome for Pausanias. Exactly what happened is unclear; Thucydides gives few details, although later writers added plenty of lurid insinuations.<sup id="cite_ref-Fine331_182-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fine331-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> Through his arrogance and arbitrary actions (Thucydides says "violence"), Pausanias managed to alienate many of the Allied contingents, particularly those that had just been freed from Persian overlordship.<sup id="cite_ref-Sea242_181-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sea242-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fine331_182-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fine331-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TI95_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TI95-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> The Ionians and others asked the Athenians to take leadership of the campaign, to which they agreed.<sup id="cite_ref-TI95_183-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TI95-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> The Spartans, hearing of his behaviour, recalled Pausanias and tried him on charges of collaborating with the enemy. Although he was acquitted, his reputation was tarnished and he was not restored to his command.<sup id="cite_ref-TI95_183-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TI95-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Pausanias returned to Byzantium as a private citizen in 477 BC, and took command of the city until he was expelled by the Athenians. He then crossed the <a href="/wiki/Bosporus" title="Bosporus">Bosporus</a> and settled in <a href="/wiki/Kolonai" title="Kolonai">Kolonai</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Troad" title="Troad">Troad</a>, until he was again accused of collaborating with the Persians and was recalled by the Spartans for a trial after which he starved himself to death.<sup id="cite_ref-Fine338*_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fine338*-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> The timescale is unclear, but Pausanias may have remained in possession of Byzantium until 470 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-Fine338*_184-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fine338*-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the meantime, the Spartans had sent <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dorkis&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Dorkis (page does not exist)">Dorkis</a> to Byzantium with a small force, to take command of the Allied force. However, he found that the rest of the Allies were no longer prepared to accept Spartan leadership, and therefore returned home.<sup id="cite_ref-TI95_183-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TI95-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Wars_of_the_Delian_League_.28477.E2.80.93449_BC.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Wars_of_the_Delian_League_(477–449_BC)">Wars of the Delian League (477–449 BC)</span></h2>
<div role="navigation" class="navbox" aria-labelledby="Wars_of_the_Delian_League" style="margin:0;float:right;clear:right;width:25.5em;margin-bottom:0.5em;margin-left:1em;;padding:3px"><table class="nowraplinks navbox-vertical mw-collapsible autocollapse navbox-inner" style="border-spacing:0;background:transparent;color:inherit"><tbody><tr><th scope="col" class="navbox-title" colspan="2" style="background-color:#C3D6EF;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Campaignbox_Wars_of_the_Delian_League" title="Template:Campaignbox Wars of the Delian League"><abbr title="View this template" style=";background-color:#C3D6EF;;background:none transparent;border:none;-moz-box-shadow:none;-webkit-box-shadow:none;box-shadow:none; padding:0;">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Campaignbox_Wars_of_the_Delian_League" title="Template talk:Campaignbox Wars of the Delian League"><abbr title="Discuss this template" style=";background-color:#C3D6EF;;background:none transparent;border:none;-moz-box-shadow:none;-webkit-box-shadow:none;box-shadow:none; padding:0;">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Campaignbox_Wars_of_the_Delian_League&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template" style=";background-color:#C3D6EF;;background:none transparent;border:none;-moz-box-shadow:none;-webkit-box-shadow:none;box-shadow:none; padding:0;">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div><div id="Wars_of_the_Delian_League" style="font-size:114%;margin:0 4em"><span style="line-height:1.6em"><a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Delian_League" title="Wars of the Delian League">Wars of the <br /> Delian League</a></span></div></th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="navbox-list navbox-odd hlist" style="width:100%;padding:0px"><div style="padding:0em 0.25em">

</div></td></tr></tbody></table></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Delian_League">Delian League</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Delian_League" title="Delian League">Delian League</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_athenian_empire_431_BC-en.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Map_athenian_empire_431_BC-en.svg/250px-Map_athenian_empire_431_BC-en.svg.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Map_athenian_empire_431_BC-en.svg/375px-Map_athenian_empire_431_BC-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Map_athenian_empire_431_BC-en.svg/500px-Map_athenian_empire_431_BC-en.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="993" data-file-height="794" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_athenian_empire_431_BC-en.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Athens and her "empire" in 431 BC. The empire was the direct descendant of the <a href="/wiki/Delian_League" title="Delian League">Delian League</a></div></div></div>
<p>After Byzantium, the Spartans were allegedly eager to end their involvement in the war. The Spartans were supposedly of the view that, with the liberation of mainland Greece and the Greek cities of Asia Minor, the war's purpose had already been reached. There was also perhaps a feeling that securing long-term security for the Asian Greeks would prove impossible.<sup id="cite_ref-h362_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h362-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> In the aftermath of Mycale, the Spartan king <a href="/wiki/Leotychides" class="mw-redirect" title="Leotychides">Leotychides</a> had proposed transplanting all the Greeks from Asia Minor to Europe as the only method of permanently freeing them from Persian dominion. <a href="/wiki/Xanthippus" title="Xanthippus">Xanthippus</a>, the Athenian commander at Mycale, had furiously rejected this; the Ionian cities were originally Athenian colonies, and the Athenians, if no-one else, would protect the Ionians.<sup id="cite_ref-h362_185-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h362-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> This marks the point at which the leadership of the Greek Alliance effectively passed to the Athenians.<sup id="cite_ref-h362_185-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h362-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> With the Spartan withdrawal after Byzantium, the leadership of the Athenians became explicit.
</p><p>The loose alliance of city-states that had fought against Xerxes's invasion had been dominated by Sparta and the Peloponnesian league. With the withdrawal of these states, a congress was called on the holy island of <a href="/wiki/Delos" title="Delos">Delos</a> to institute a new alliance to continue the fight against the Persians. This alliance, now including many of the Aegean islands, was formally constituted as the
'First Athenian Alliance', commonly known as the <a href="/wiki/Delian_League" title="Delian League">Delian League</a>. According to Thucydides, the official aim of the League was to "avenge the wrongs they suffered by ravaging the territory of the king".<sup id="cite_ref-TI96_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TI96-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> In reality, this goal was divided into three main efforts—to prepare for future invasion, to seek revenge against Persia, and to organize a means of dividing spoils of war. The members were given a choice of either supplying armed forces or paying a tax to the joint treasury; most states chose the tax.<sup id="cite_ref-TI96_186-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TI96-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Campaigns_against_Persia">Campaigns against Persia</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Delian_League" title="Wars of the Delian League">Wars of the Delian League</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Delian_League_Campaign_Map.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Delian_League_Campaign_Map.png/300px-Delian_League_Campaign_Map.png" decoding="async" width="300" height="300" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Delian_League_Campaign_Map.png/450px-Delian_League_Campaign_Map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bb/Delian_League_Campaign_Map.png 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Delian_League_Campaign_Map.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map showing the locations of battles fought by the Delian League, 477–449 BC</div></div></div>
<p>Throughout the 470s BC, the Delian League campaigned in Thrace and the Aegean to remove the remaining Persian garrisons from the region, primarily under the command of the Athenian politician <a href="/wiki/Cimon" title="Cimon">Cimon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Sea250_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sea250-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> In the early part of the next decade, Cimon began campaigning in <a href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>, seeking to strengthen the Greek position there.<sup id="cite_ref-PC12_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PC12-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> At the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Eurymedon" title="Battle of the Eurymedon">Battle of the Eurymedon</a> in <a href="/wiki/Pamphylia" title="Pamphylia">Pamphylia</a>, the Athenians and allied fleet achieved a stunning double victory, destroying a Persian fleet and then landing the ships' marines to attack and rout the Persian army. After this battle, the Persians took an essentially passive role in the conflict, anxious not to risk battle if possible.<sup id="cite_ref-PC13_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PC13-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Towards the end of the 460s BC, the Athenians took the ambitious decision to support a revolt in the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egyptian</a> <a href="/wiki/Satrapy" class="mw-redirect" title="Satrapy">satrapy</a> of the Persian empire. Although the Greek task force achieved initial successes, they were unable to capture the Persian garrison in <a href="/wiki/Memphis,_Egypt" title="Memphis, Egypt">Memphis</a>, despite a three-year long siege.<sup id="cite_ref-TI104_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TI104-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> The Persians then counterattacked, and the Athenian force was itself besieged for 18 months, before being wiped out.<sup id="cite_ref-TI109_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TI109-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> This disaster, coupled with <a href="/wiki/First_Peloponnesian_War" title="First Peloponnesian War">ongoing warfare</a> in Greece, dissuaded the Athenians from resuming conflict with Persia.<sup id="cite_ref-Sea271*_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sea271*-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> In 451 BC however, a truce was agreed in Greece, and Cimon was then able to lead an expedition to Cyprus. However, while besieging <a href="/wiki/Kition" title="Kition">Kition</a>, Cimon died, and the Athenian force decided to withdraw, winning another double victory at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Salamis_(in_Cyprus)" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Salamis (in Cyprus)">Battle of Salamis-in-Cyprus</a> in order to extricate themselves.<sup id="cite_ref-TI112_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TI112-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> This campaign marked the end of hostilities between the Delian League and Persia, and therefore the end of the Greco-Persian Wars.<sup id="cite_ref-PC19_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PC19-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Peace_with_Persia">Peace with Persia</span></h2>
<p>After the Battle of Salamis-in-Cyprus, Thucydides makes no further mention of conflict with the Persians, saying that the Greeks simply returned home.<sup id="cite_ref-TI112_193-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TI112-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> Diodorus, on the other hand, claims that in the aftermath of Salamis, a full-blown peace treaty (the "Peace of Callias") was agreed with the Persians.<sup id="cite_ref-DSXII4_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DSXII4-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> Diodorus was probably following the history of <a href="/wiki/Ephorus" title="Ephorus">Ephorus</a> at this point, who in turn was presumably influenced by his teacher <a href="/wiki/Isocrates" title="Isocrates">Isocrates</a>—from whom there is the earliest reference to the supposed peace, in 380 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-Fine360_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fine360-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Even during the 4th century BC, the idea of the treaty was controversial, and two authors from that period, <a href="/wiki/Callisthenes" title="Callisthenes">Callisthenes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Theopompus" title="Theopompus">Theopompus</a>, appear to reject its existence.<sup id="cite_ref-Sea280_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sea280-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It is possible that the Athenians had attempted to negotiate with the Persians previously. Plutarch suggests that in the aftermath of the victory at the Eurymedon, <a href="/wiki/Artaxerxes_I_of_Persia" title="Artaxerxes I of Persia">Artaxerxes</a> had agreed a peace treaty with the Greeks, even naming Callias as the Athenian ambassador involved. However, as Plutarch admits, Callisthenes denied that such a peace was made at this point (c. 466 BC).<sup id="cite_ref-PC13_189-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PC13-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> Herodotus also mentions, in passing, an Athenian embassy headed by <a href="/wiki/Callias" class="mw-redirect" title="Callias">Callias</a>, which was sent to <a href="/wiki/Susa" title="Susa">Susa</a> to negotiate with Artaxerxes.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> This embassy included some <a href="/wiki/Argos" title="Argos">Argive</a> representatives and can probably be therefore dated to c. 461 BC (after an alliance was agreed between Athens and Argos).<sup id="cite_ref-Fine360_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fine360-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> This embassy may have been an attempt to reach some kind of peace agreement, and it has even been suggested that the failure of these hypothetical negotiations led to the Athenian decision to support the Egyptian revolt.<sup id="cite_ref-Kag84_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kag84-198">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> The ancient sources therefore disagree as to whether there was an official peace or not, and, if there was, when it was agreed.
</p><p>Opinion amongst modern historians is also split; for instance, Fine accepts the concept of the Peace of Callias,<sup id="cite_ref-Fine360_21-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fine360-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> whereas Sealey effectively rejects it.<sup id="cite_ref-Sea281_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sea281-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> Holland accepts that some kind of accommodation was made between Athens and Persia, but no actual treaty.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> Fine argues that Callisthenes's denial that a treaty was made after the Eurymedon does not preclude a peace being made at another point. Further, he suggests that Theopompus was actually referring to a treaty that had allegedly been negotiated with Persia in 423 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-Fine360_21-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fine360-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> If these views are correct, it would remove one major obstacle to the acceptance of the treaty's existence. A further argument for the existence of the treaty is the sudden withdrawal of the Athenians from Cyprus in 449 BC, which Fine suggests makes most sense in the light of some kind of peace agreement.<sup id="cite_ref-Fine363_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fine363-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> On the other hand, if there was indeed some kind of accommodation, Thucydides's failure to mention it is odd. In his digression on the <i>pentekontaetia</i>, his aim is to explain the growth of Athenian power, and such a treaty, and the fact that the Delian allies were not released from their obligations after it, would have marked a major step in the Athenian ascendancy.<sup id="cite_ref-Sea282_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sea282-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> Conversely, it has been suggested that certain passages elsewhere in Thucydides's history are best interpreted as referring to a peace agreement.<sup id="cite_ref-Fine360_21-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fine360-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> There is thus no clear consensus amongst modern historians as to the treaty's existence.
</p><p>The ancient sources that give details of the treaty are reasonably consistent in their description of the terms:<sup id="cite_ref-Fine360_21-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fine360-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DSXII4_195-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DSXII4-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sea280_196-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sea280-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>All Greek cities of Asia were to 'live by their own laws' <i>or</i> 'be autonomous' (depending on translation).</li>
<li>Persian satraps (and presumably their armies) were not to travel west of the <a href="/wiki/Halys_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Halys River">Halys River</a> (<a href="/wiki/Isocrates" title="Isocrates">Isocrates</a>) <i>or</i> closer than a day's journey on horseback to the Aegean Sea (<a href="/wiki/Callisthenes" title="Callisthenes">Callisthenes</a>) <i>or</i> closer than three days' journey on foot to the Aegean Sea (<a href="/wiki/Ephorus" title="Ephorus">Ephorus</a> and Diodorus).</li>
<li>No Persian warship was to sail west of <a href="/wiki/Phaselis" title="Phaselis">Phaselis</a> (on the southern coast of Asia Minor), nor west of the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Cyanaean_rocks&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Cyanaean rocks (page does not exist)">Cyanaean rocks</a> (probably at the eastern end of the <a href="/wiki/Bosporus" title="Bosporus">Bosporus</a>, on the north coast).</li>
<li>If the terms were observed by the king and his generals, then the Athenians were not to send troops to lands ruled by Persia.</li></ul>
<p>From the Persian perspective, such terms would not be so humiliating as they might at first seem. The Persians already allowed the Greek cities of Asia to be governed under their own laws (under the reorganization conducted by <a href="/wiki/Artaphernes" title="Artaphernes">Artaphernes</a>, following the <a href="/wiki/Ionian_Revolt" title="Ionian Revolt">Ionian Revolt</a>). By these terms, the Ionians were still Persian subjects, as they had been. Furthermore, Athens had already demonstrated their superiority at sea at the Eurymedon and Salamis-in-Cyprus, so any legal limitations for the Persian fleet were nothing more than "de jure" recognition of military realities. In exchange for limiting the movement of Persian troops in one region of the realm, Artaxerxes secured a promise from the Athenians to stay out of his entire realm.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Aftermath_and_later_conflicts">Aftermath and later conflicts</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DYNASTS_of_LYCIA._Kherei._Circa_440-30-410_BC.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/DYNASTS_of_LYCIA._Kherei._Circa_440-30-410_BC.jpg/300px-DYNASTS_of_LYCIA._Kherei._Circa_440-30-410_BC.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/DYNASTS_of_LYCIA._Kherei._Circa_440-30-410_BC.jpg/450px-DYNASTS_of_LYCIA._Kherei._Circa_440-30-410_BC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/DYNASTS_of_LYCIA._Kherei._Circa_440-30-410_BC.jpg/600px-DYNASTS_of_LYCIA._Kherei._Circa_440-30-410_BC.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="417" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DYNASTS_of_LYCIA._Kherei._Circa_440-30-410_BC.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dynast of <a href="/wiki/Lycia" title="Lycia">Lycia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kherei" title="Kherei">Kherei</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Athena" title="Athena">Athena</a> on the obverse, and himself wearing the Persian cap on the reverse. Circa 440/30–410 BC.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CILICIA,_Soloi._Tiribazos,_Satrap_of_Lydia._Second_reign,_388-380_BC.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/CILICIA%2C_Soloi._Tiribazos%2C_Satrap_of_Lydia._Second_reign%2C_388-380_BC.jpg/300px-CILICIA%2C_Soloi._Tiribazos%2C_Satrap_of_Lydia._Second_reign%2C_388-380_BC.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/CILICIA%2C_Soloi._Tiribazos%2C_Satrap_of_Lydia._Second_reign%2C_388-380_BC.jpg/450px-CILICIA%2C_Soloi._Tiribazos%2C_Satrap_of_Lydia._Second_reign%2C_388-380_BC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/CILICIA%2C_Soloi._Tiribazos%2C_Satrap_of_Lydia._Second_reign%2C_388-380_BC.jpg/600px-CILICIA%2C_Soloi._Tiribazos%2C_Satrap_of_Lydia._Second_reign%2C_388-380_BC.jpg 2x" data-file-width="650" data-file-height="350" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CILICIA,_Soloi._Tiribazos,_Satrap_of_Lydia._Second_reign,_388-380_BC.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coinage of <a href="/wiki/Tiribazos" class="mw-redirect" title="Tiribazos">Tiribazos</a>, Satrap of <a href="/wiki/Lydia" title="Lydia">Lydia</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Ahuramazda" class="mw-redirect" title="Ahuramazda">Ahuramazda</a> on the obverse. 388–380 BC.</div></div></div>
<p>Towards the end of the conflict with Persia, the process by which the <a href="/wiki/Delian_League" title="Delian League">Delian League</a> became the Athenian Empire reached its conclusion.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> The allies of Athens were not released from their obligations to provide either money or ships, despite the cessation of hostilities.<sup id="cite_ref-Sea282_202-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sea282-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> In Greece, the <a href="/wiki/First_Peloponnesian_War" title="First Peloponnesian War">First Peloponnesian War</a> between the power-blocs of Athens and Sparta, which had continued on/off since 460 BC, finally ended in 445 BC, with the agreement of a thirty-year truce.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> However, the growing enmity between Sparta and Athens would lead, just 14 years later, into the outbreak of the Second <a href="/wiki/Peloponnesian_War" title="Peloponnesian War">Peloponnesian War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> This disastrous conflict, which dragged on for 27 years, would eventually result in the utter destruction of Athenian power, the dismemberment of the Athenian empire, and the establishment of a <a href="/wiki/Spartan_hegemony" title="Spartan hegemony">Spartan hegemony</a> over Greece.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> However, not just Athens suffered—the conflict would significantly weaken the whole of Greece.<sup id="cite_ref-Dand256_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dand256-207">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Repeatedly defeated in battle by the Greeks, and plagued by internal rebellions that hindered their ability to fight the Greeks, after 449 BC, <a href="/wiki/Artaxerxes_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Artaxerxes I">Artaxerxes I</a> and his successors instead adopted a policy of divide-and-rule.<sup id="cite_ref-Dand256_207-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dand256-207">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> Avoiding fighting the Greeks themselves, the Persians instead attempted to set Athens against Sparta, regularly bribing politicians to achieve their aims. In this way, they ensured that the Greeks remained distracted by internal conflicts, and were unable to turn their attentions to Persia.<sup id="cite_ref-Dand256_207-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dand256-207">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> There was no open conflict between the Greeks and Persia until 396 BC, when the Spartan king <a href="/wiki/Agesilaus" class="mw-redirect" title="Agesilaus">Agesilaus</a> briefly invaded Asia Minor; as Plutarch points out, the Greeks were far too busy overseeing the destruction of their own power to fight against the "barbarians".<sup id="cite_ref-PC19_194-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PC19-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>If the wars of the Delian League shifted the balance of power between Greece and Persia in favour of the Greeks, then the subsequent half-century of internecine conflict in Greece did much to restore the balance of power to Persia. The Persians entered the Peloponnesian War in 411 BC forming a mutual-defence pact with Sparta and combining their naval resources against Athens in exchange for sole Persian control of Ionia.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> In 404 BC when <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Younger" title="Cyrus the Younger">Cyrus the Younger</a> attempted to seize the Persian throne, he recruited <a href="/wiki/Ten_Thousand_(Greek_mercenaries)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ten Thousand (Greek mercenaries)">13,000 Greek mercenaries</a> from all over the Greek world, of which Sparta sent 700–800, believing they were following the terms of the defence pact and unaware of the army's true purpose.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> After the failure of Cyrus, Persia tried to regain control of the Ionian city-states, which had rebelled during the conflict. The Ionians refused to capitulate and called upon Sparta for assistance, which she provided, in 396–395 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> Athens, however, sided with the Persians, which led in turn to another large-scale conflict in Greece, the <a href="/wiki/Corinthian_War" title="Corinthian War">Corinthian War</a>. Towards the end of that conflict, in 387 BC, Sparta sought the aid of Persia to shore up her position. Under the so-called <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Antalcidas" title="Peace of Antalcidas">"King's Peace"</a> that brought the war to an end, <a href="/wiki/Artaxerxes_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Artaxerxes II">Artaxerxes II</a> demanded and received the return of the cities of Asia Minor from the Spartans, in return for which the Persians threatened to make war on any Greek state that did not make peace.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> This humiliating treaty, which undid all the Greek gains of the previous century, sacrificed the Greeks of Asia Minor so that the Spartans could maintain their hegemony over Greece.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> It is in the aftermath of this treaty that Greek orators began to refer to the Peace of Callias (whether fictional or not), as a counterpoint to the shame of the King's Peace, and a glorious example of the "good old days" when the Greeks of the Aegean had been freed from Persian rule by the Delian League.<sup id="cite_ref-Fine360_21-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fine360-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<p><span id="cnote_i"><b><a href="#ref_i">^</a></b></span>&#160;<b>i:</b>&#160;The exact period covered by the term "Greco-Persian Wars" is open to interpretation, and usage varies between academics; the <a href="/wiki/Ionian_Revolt" title="Ionian Revolt">Ionian Revolt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Delian_League" title="Wars of the Delian League">Wars of the Delian League</a> are sometimes excluded. This article covers the maximum extent of the wars.<br />
<span id="cnote_ii"><b><a href="#ref_ii">^</a></b></span>&#160;<b>ii:</b>&#160;Archaeological evidence for the Panionion before the 6th century BC is very weak, and possibly this temple was a relatively late development.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup><br />
<span id="cnote_iii"><b><a href="#ref_iii">^</a></b></span>&#160;<b>iii:</b>&#160;Although historically inaccurate, the legend of a Greek messenger running to Athens with news of the victory and then promptly expiring, became the inspiration for this athletics event, introduced at the 1896 Athens Olympics, and originally run between Marathon and Athens.<sup id="cite_ref-h198_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h198-214">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup><br />
</p>
